```
On Wed, 19 Oct 2005 18:39:01 GMT, "Oliver Wong" <owong@castortech.com>
wrote:

>Lin: Ba...Batou!
>Batou: You say my name without -san or -sama?
>Lin: I'm sorry, I didn't do it, Batou-san!
>Batou: I'm too good to be addressed merely as "-san" by you!

Without knowing the subject matter, the concept was clear to me.

I've been reading a (very fun) series of SF novels that are basically
Patrick O'Brian novels in space.   The author, David Drake puts an
apology/explanation in the forward for using current systems of
measurement.   But he also creates dialog where familiarity with the
British class structure is useful.   Enough time has happened so that
we know they won't talk like us anyway.     I don't see that that
explanation was necessary.    

The toughest part about translation is when people think differently.
We see some of that when changing to OO programming.   Things don't
translate directly.
 
We don't convert systems, we re-write systems.    The benefit of being
able to recompile our CoBOL in the new system doesn't count for much
when the new system has a completely different data design, and a
completely different work flow.
```

```
> news:h71dl116up2dji42la76tipbgk5e8ptijo@4ax.com...

> > syllables to not make the mouth movements too silly.

Agreed, though there are some interesting cases.  In anime, for
example, dialogue is often recorded after the animation is done, so
there's no attempt to make the mouth "flaps" (as they're called)
agree with the actual spoken syllables.  When anime gets dubbed for
US viewers, however, the translators often *do* try to get the flaps
to agree with the audio, so the dub track may match the video better
than the original does.

>     In the end, we decided that our target audience were the "Otakus" 
> (hardcore fans) who would be more interested in a "purist" rendition of the 
> story, and we ended up putting a few "translator's notes" sprinkled about 
> during those scenes when no dialog was taking place.

Ah, the always-popular translator's notes.  AnimEigo is famous for
inserting such notes to accompany the subtitles on some of their
releases, such as "Kimagure Orange Road".  I've also seen them on
some fansubs - IIRC, "Kodomo no Omocha" has them, for example.
```

- **From:** James Johnson <saildot.maryland@verizon.net>
- **Date:** 2005-10-23T01:13:16+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<h97jl157arv13g5ovetvhdhp11losrr2ak@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<9OV3f.23230$S4.19969@edtnps84> <20051015132907.058$g2@news.newsreader.com> <pan.2005.10.16.03.08.37.117719@starband.net> <11l5em9nkca7318@news.supernews.com> <dj0guj0k2f@news4.newsguy.com> <hrn7l1daddpu1m368c3mpievfad47tt24v@4ax.com> <1129570813.497500.139530@z14g2000cwz.googlegroups.com> <zqT4f.35459$Lp.18470@bignews5.bellsouth.net>`

```
On Mon, 17 Oct 2005 15:03:40 -0500, "Judson McClendon" <judmc@sunvaley0.com>
wrote:

>"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
>>
>> Legibility is entirely what one is used to.  ...


Not necessarily.  A lot of it was the culture of the code shops.  

COBOL shops emphasized documentation in code and controls because this code was
controlling/directing big money throughout the corporation.  And that people
were going to have to maintain and modify the code was usually understood from
the very beginning. And undocumented code took longer to fix/modify and
therefore wasted the business's money.  Its roots are in business processes.

C and C++ and its syntactical cousins originated in research labs and grad
student programs where code elegance and sparseness were emphasized. And if the
code had some cute function that wasn't obvious then that was better still in
the contest of "who is the most skilled programmer".  This resulted in a bias
against documentation as it was against the unwritten standard - that if you
need documentation to understand it then you don't measure up, and putting
documentation in was an insult against good coders.

JJ

>
>Not entirely! To a large degree, yes. Not all languages are equally 
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
>-----------------------------

James Johnson
remove the "dot" from after sail in email address to reply
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol work?

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** John Culleton <john@wexfordpress.com>
- **Date:** 2005-10-19T20:59:48+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<OZadnYRVUOPeKcveRVn-ig@adelphia.com>`
- **References:** `<9OV3f.23230$S4.19969@edtnps84> <20051015132907.058$g2@news.newsreader.com> <pan.2005.10.16.03.08.37.117719@starband.net> <11l5em9nkca7318@news.supernews.com> <dj0guj0k2f@news4.newsguy.com> <hrn7l1daddpu1m368c3mpievfad47tt24v@4ax.com>`

```
Ian Dalziel wrote:

> On 17 Oct 2005 15:45:23 GMT, mwojcik@newsguy.com (Michael Wojcik)
> wrote:
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> More to the point, have you seen any C++ code that was legible?

The matter of style is important here. It is perfectly possible to write in 
COBOL
ADD A, B giving C
or even
COMPUTE (C = A + B)

which looks like a FORTRAN family language but you are far more likely to
see

ADD DOLLAR-AMOUNT, SALES-TAX GIVING TOTAL-CHARGES.

COBOL encourages the use of meaningful names, and other languages, at least
the way they are usually taught, don't.  

COBOL also encourages meaningful internal documentation, starting with the
(original) IDENTIFICATION DIVISION. Other languages have commenting
mechanisms, but those languages are neutral on the subject. And internal
documentation is not part of the usual teaching method.

So IMO culture as much as specific capabilities encourages readable COBOL.
That was a principal goal of GMH when she conceived the language.   
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol work?

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** "Oliver Wong" <owong@castortech.com>
- **Date:** 2005-10-19T21:56:16+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4iz5f.37655$S4.20966@edtnps84>`
- **References:** `<9OV3f.23230$S4.19969@edtnps84> <20051015132907.058$g2@news.newsreader.com> <pan.2005.10.16.03.08.37.117719@starband.net> <11l5em9nkca7318@news.supernews.com> <dj0guj0k2f@news4.newsguy.com> <hrn7l1daddpu1m368c3mpievfad47tt24v@4ax.com> <OZadnYRVUOPeKcveRVn-ig@adelphia.com>`

```
"John Culleton" <john@wexfordpress.com> wrote in message 
news:OZadnYRVUOPeKcveRVn-ig@adelphia.com...
> COBOL encourages the use of meaningful names, and other languages, at 
> least
> the way they are usually taught, don't.

    I'm not sure what you mean by this; I've never heard a computer science 
teacher say "Don't use meaningful names", while I have heard several 
computer science teachers say "(Do) use meaningful names". Some of then even 
go so far as to take off marks if your program is "difficult to understand" 
in their (incontestable) opinion.

> COBOL also encourages meaningful internal documentation, starting with the
> (original) IDENTIFICATION DIVISION. Other languages have commenting
> mechanisms, but those languages are neutral on the subject. And internal
> documentation is not part of the usual teaching method.

    This is true. For many languages I'm familiar with, a documentation 
system is usually processed by a third party application, and not the 
compiler (e.g. JavaDoc, Doxygen, C#'s XML documentation system, etc.) 
However, it seems that the developper's toolset is moving more and more 
towards intelligent IDEs, where it starts to become difficult to know where 
one tool (e.g. the compiler) ends and another (e.g. the documentation 
processor) begins.

> So IMO culture as much as specific capabilities encourages readable COBOL.
> That was a principal goal of GMH when she conceived the language.

    As an aside, I was surprised to discover that there actually did exist a 
language which seemed to DIScourage meaningful names: The GPSS language had 
"arbitrary" lengths for function names and variable (I forget the exact 
figures, but it was something like all names had to be between 4 and 8 
characters long), and restrictions such as variables whose type were integer 
had to start with either I, J or K, and variables whose type were real had 
to start with X, Y or Z (all other variables were assumed to be Strings).

    I say "arbitrary", but the reason laid in the fact that GPSS programs 
were originally written on fixed-grid cards, with column-based areas like 
COBOL.

    Assuming you wanted an integer, after the I/J/K, you only had 7 
characters left with which to name the variable, so variable names were very 
rarely meaningful in GPSS programs I've seen and written. To make up for it, 
my GPSS programs were often documented liberally. In fact, the combined size 
of just the comments often exceeded the size of the code itself.

    - Oliver
```

```
> > COBOL also encourages meaningful internal documentation, starting with the
> > (original) IDENTIFICATION DIVISION. Other languages have commenting
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> compiler (e.g. JavaDoc, Doxygen, C#'s XML documentation system, etc.) 

Doxygen is "third party", but Javadoc and the C# documentation system
are part of the standard toolset from the primary vendor.

While C and C++, say, don't standardize an internal documentation
mechanism, Javadoc markup *is* a standard part of Java.  Ditto for
the documentation systems of Perl and Python, for example.  COBOL is
far from unique in this regard.

And using a separate tool for generating documentation is not a
compelling objection.  My COBOL toolchain uses more than one step;
why should any other language be penalized for separating build steps
into separate processes?  In fact, I consider that a distinct
advantage of a language implementation.

> However, it seems that the developper's toolset is moving more and more 
> towards intelligent IDEs, where it starts to become difficult to know where 
> one tool (e.g. the compiler) ends and another (e.g. the documentation 
> processor) begins.

Which is precisely why I don't like IDEs - but I don't think they're
relevant to this matter anyway.  Incorporating documentation into
source code doesn't depend on IDEs in any significant way.  A
developer knows how to use the toolset or doesn't; whether it's
wrapped up in an IDE is inconsequential, at least for questions of
what tools are available.

>     As an aside, I was surprised to discover that there actually did exist a 
> language which seemed to DIScourage meaningful names: The GPSS language had 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> to start with X, Y or Z (all other variables were assumed to be Strings).

Early BASICs had similar requirements.

Strictly-conforming C programs must not exceed the minimum limits
required of all implementations, which means that in the current
(1999) standard internal identifiers have only 63 significant (case-
sensitive) initial characters, and external identifiers have only 31
significant (case-insensitive) initial characters.  That's usually
plenty, but prior to the 1999 standard the limits were 31 characters
for internal identifiers and only 6(!) significant initial case-
insensitive characters for external ones.

No one writes strictly-conforming C programs (there's a line of
argument that it's impossible to do so, because of the requirement
that a strictly-conforming program not depend on any implementation-
defined behavior), of course.  And since nearly all C implementations
treated considerably more than six characters as significant for
external identifiers, that limit is almost universally ignored.  But
you could have a conforming C implementation that treated all
external identifiers beginning with the same six characters as the
same identifier.

But that's not because C discourages meaningful names; it's because
it tries to support as many platforms as possible, including some
old, very limited ones.
```

```
--------------------------------------------------------
strcmp(s,t) /* return <0 if s<t, 0 if s==t, >0 if s>t */
char *s *t
{
  for ( ; *s ==*t; s++ t++)
  if (*s == '\0')
            return (0);
  return (*s - *t);
}
_The C Programming Language_, Kernighan & Ritchie p102
---------------------------------------------------------
set b ""
for {set i [expr [llength $a]-1]} {$1 >=0} {incr i -1{
   lappend b[lindex $a $1]
}
_Tcl and the Tk Toolkit_, John Ousterhout p74
---------------------------------------------------

an effort to use meaningful names. But the general trend is cryptic
```

```
> I have only documentary evidence, lacking paparazzi shots of
> professors' whiteboards:
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> _The C Programming Language_, Kernighan & Ritchie p102

What is not readily and immediately understandable in this ?

One of the advantages of local variables and parameters is that they
will not clash with any other part of the code.  Replacing s and t
(which are _by_convention_ used as string variable parameters) by
the_first_string and the_second_string would add nothing to the code
other  than making it unwieldy.

If you looked at strcpy it would probably have the same s and t, it is
an idiom.

I also noticed immediately on glancing at the code that you had missed
out a comma.
```

```
In Russell M. Armstrong, "Modular Progamming in
COBOL," there is a sample program (AP503) in
the Appendix. The program is 22 1/2 pages. The
first 7 1/2 pages is the REMARKS paragraph. The
procedure division is about 11 1/2 pages and NOTE
paragraphs seem to occupy about one-third of that
space. For the most part, the NOTE pargraphs
explain what the following paragraph-group does,
eventhough it is often clear from the code what is
done. I consider this to be a lot of "noise" making
the program less clear, therefore "obfuscated".
But, maybe that is just me! <g>

> Too bad COBOL doesn't have such crystal clear, never mistaken features
such
> as '=' vs '=='. And the subtleties of C-style pointers makes COBOL
> programmers pine for that type of impossible-to-misunderstand clarity.
ROTFL

Of course, the use of INSPECT CONVERTING to
rearrange characters according to their pattern is
always clear (easily understood; without ambiguity)
and obvious (lacking in subtlety)!
```

```
Rick, both your issues involve misuse of features, not features that are 
problems in and of themselves. At first glance it is obvious that use of '=' 
vs. '==' is going to be mistaken by the human eye/brain, without a doubt. 
Any programmer who denies *ever* being confused by C-style pointers either 
hasn't used them much, or is lying. :-)

It's not a clarity issue, but whoever selected colon-equal (:=) as an 
assignment operator was insane. For such a common typed sequence to be not 
only two characters, but one of them shifted as well - that's insanity. 
Reminds me of the QWERTY keyboard - purposefully designed to be inefficient 
so the typists of the 1890's wouldn't overrun the machines of the day 
(fact). :-)
```

```

"Judson McClendon" <judmc@sunvaley0.com> wrote in message
news:i669f.32028$Pp1.6272@bignews3.bellsouth.net...
> "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote:
> > "Judson McClendon" <judmc@sunvaley0.com> wrote:
…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
> problems in and of themselves.

What are the objective criteria for determining proper vs.
improper use?

Armstrong went to great lengths justifying the content
of the REMARKS and NOTE paragraphs. Nearly
twenty years later, Steve McConnell, "Code Complete,"
supplied critera, that distinguishs between "intent" (proper)
and "how" (improper). Perhaps there is something in
between these two. For my part, I suspect that a form
of hysteresis is affecting me. I have seen so much excess
in the past that I have difficulty accepting any "noise" in
the present.

Any particular use of INSPECT CONVERTING to
rearrange characters may be confusing, when taken out
of context; but easily understood in context. If a
particular use is clear, how can that be a misuse?

> At first glance it is obvious that use of '='
> vs. '==' is going to be mistaken by the human eye/brain, without a doubt.

I went into Visual Studio (for the first time in months)
and entered both an assignment statement and a test
for equality. The character set used by Visual Studio
causes a gap to appear between the two symbols used
for equality. There can be no misstake between one
and two occurrences. It's like '=' and '= ='. Furthermore,
tests for equality always occur within parenthesis:
assignment only occasionally. I think this may reduce
the possibility of mistaking one for the other.

> Any programmer who denies *ever* being confused by C-style pointers either
> hasn't used them much, or is lying. :-)

Well, I admit to having been confused by such
pointers, when used by others, but the confusion was
not long-lasting.

> It's not a clarity issue, but whoever selected colon-equal (:=) as an
> assignment operator was insane. For such a common typed sequence to be not
> only two characters, but one of them shifted as well - that's insanity.

I absolutely hate the Pascal and Ada assignment operator.
Years ago, I read a book on Ada by Wegner and two
others. While reading the passage on the assignment
operator, I got a distinct mental image of three geezers
sitting in rocking chairs on a porch nodding their heads
while agreeing with each other and, seemingly, without
a clue as to how much the operator was hated by others.
Of course, we others, resolved the problem by switching
to other languages, like C.
```

```

"Ian Dalziel" <iandalziel@lineone.net> wrote in message
news:cr7am1p5bsjhevmu1rqihg7bonlsuqvt68@4ax.com...
> On Sun, 30 Oct 2005 14:13:36 -0500, "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
> wrote:
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
> enlightening comment "Load XFPSIG".

Edward Yourdon, in "Techniques of Program Structure
and Design," 1975, page 11, reports of a programmer
who used an octal constant with only comment in the
entire program:

CONST23:    3443     ; R.I.P.L.V.B.

Steve McConnell, in "Code Complete," 1993, page 468,
reports of a programmer who used an instruction with the
only comment in the entire program:

    MOV  AX,723h     ; R.I.P.L.V.B.

One may be suspicious of this coincidence. Maybe the
same programmer, maybe a "copycat", or maybe
embellishment; but the complaint is the same by both
authors. The use of a value and an acknowledgement
of the date of Beethoven's death in octal, hexadecimal,
or even decimal (1827), is not a helpful comment.
```

```
Best, and most "helpful" comment I've ever seen was in part of the

assembler there was a little box, carefully marked out in asterisks,

"There's pleasure sure in being mad

assembler code for LEO 3's O/S that declared...

"When I first wrote this code, only God and I knew what I was doing...
  Now, God alone knows.".
```

```
I once ran into a particularly obtuse stretch of ALGOL code, running some 
500 lines.  Right in the middle, indented to about column 60, was the lone 
comment in the entire block of logic:  "Yes, this is what you think it is." 
Took me quite a while figure out what I thought it was.  Took me rather less 
time to figure out that the raison d'etre of the entire block of code was 
founded on incorrect premises, upon which conclusion I deleted the entire 
block.
```

```
.    On  30.10.05
  wrote  ricksmith@mfi.net (Rick Smith)
     on  /COMP/LANG/COBOL
     in  11ma4kp1gguu036@corp.supernews.com
  about  Re: Cobol work?


>> It's not a clarity issue, but whoever selected colon-equal (:=) as an
>> assignment operator was insane. For such a common typed sequence to be
>> not only two characters, but one of them shifted as well - that's
>> insanity.

   They have both to be shifted. You have the wrong keyboard.

   Brackts have to be shifted, too, and underscores.

   But whoever invented the syntactical use of square brackets or  
curly brackets must have been insane, because those require the use of  
the left hand for characters in the right hand portion of the  
keyboard, since the right hand has to press the AltGr key.

RS> I absolutely hate the Pascal and Ada assignment operator.

   That is one of the best improvements and it dates still from Algol  
times.

   In school everybody learns that the "equal" sign "=" means that the  
two sides of the equation have the same value. And value _assignment_  
is something completely different. It changes the equation.
```

```
In article <A9d9f.19153$_31.4019@bignews5.bellsouth.net>,
 "Judson McClendon" <judmc@sunvaley0.com> wrote:

> "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
> >>
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> other factors being equal. This is patently obvious. At least to me.

Then you should not be arguing that C is complex.  C is as close to a 
one-to-one machine instruction to source instruction you are going to 
find.  

There are no subtle things in the language other than what a programmer 
choses to build in.  Any questions can be quickly addressed by a 
compiler reference manual...

Cobol, on the other hand, is loaded with 'subtle' issues.  When and what 
storage is initialized, how group moves function, intermediate numerics 
causing problems with arithmetic, when is a period just a period and 
when does it terminate a control structure...the list goes on.
```

```
2.  Not used them much
3.  Liars

them much' (ie being unfamiliar with) is more likely to lead to being
in the group 'confused' than in a separate group. That is: being in
group 1 may be the cause of being in group 2, or vice versa.  Not being
in group 1 is more likely (not less) to lead to not being in group 2.
```

```
"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:1130734689.510562.161760@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com...

I am responding in a not entirely serious manner.

>> I'd appreciate the same leniency you give apparently give yourself.
>
> """everyone else must be (or they are lying about it)."""
>
> Quite right, I should have said 'everyone else who is a programmer'.
Close: it's everyone else who is a programmer who denies *ever* being 
confused by C-style pointer

> Judson divided all programmers (from which _any_ programmer can be
> taken) into 3 groups:
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> 3.  Liars
Your groups are all wrong.  There are two sets from the group "programmers":

Group A: Programmers who deny ever being confused
Group B: Programmers who do not deny being ever confused

A literal reading of his post indicates one general deduction applied to 
only the first group:
All members of Group A haven't used pointers or are liars.

He doesn't state one thing or the other about group b though I doubt it 
would be disparaging as I inferred he was in the group by virtue of being 
"confused" at least once and not afraid to say so.

> I don't fit into groups 1 or 2, so in effect he calls me a liar.

I believe you could correct here - but wouldn't it be simpler and more fun 
to admit that just once you had been confused? That maybe by accident you 
dereferenced something and got a core dump - or maybe once you passed a 
pointer into the interlanguage COBOL call instead of a pointer to a 
pointer....or maybe you misread the declaration char* char1, char2 or forgot 
to initialize the pointer....or didn't quite get your lpsz set correctly.

The important word here though is *ever* because if you were confused at the 
beginning of your programming in C with C-style pointers - even if later you 
don't even miss a beat at statements like:

int (*pf) (int) = &f;
or
while ((psBuffer[nBufferCount++]=**psWord)&&(*++*psWord!=')'));

the fact is that you were confused when you didn't have familiarity.  If you 
were to read this with one week of C behind you the statement would be VERY 
confusing.

We just need to add a new conclusion:
All members of Group A haven't used pointers or are liars, or are just 
incredibly gifted.

I understand your points, I just think you take it all too personally...I 
don't think Judson really thinks that you are a "liar" and even if he did, 
it's probably a "white liar" and even more than that, it's not something 
that is a statement about your character...the :-) makes that clear...it was 
just a generalization that is in fact, in some cases, true.  The concepts 
aren't confusing, it's the care of the person using the concepts that makes 
the confusion ultimately. Some people do not understand array pointer 
equivalence...I don't know why...the same reason some people cannot solve 
for x and others cannot understand Shakespearean English.

>I also dispute his grouping (which he 'stands by'), because 'not having 
>used
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> in group 1 is more likely (not less) to lead to not being in group 2.
I probably agree here....so off I go into digression:

There are people that analyse data like this and it is actually quite 
fascinating.  They take accumulator bets when there is a 
miscalculation...for example:  Kobe Bryant to be the game's high scorer and 
Lakers to win.  It is much more likely that the Lakers will win if Kobe is 
the game's highest scorer and occasionally there is a slip in the odds.  So 
maybe it's 2-1 for part A and 2-1 for part B and combined the odds are 4-1 
instead of 5-2.....it's still gambling, but the odds are stacking in your 
favour.

This went on in England on the soccer matches for a long time where a very 
common bet was something like "David Beckham to score first goal and 
Manchester to win 2-0".  There were apparently some very *gifted* 
mathemeticians who would find the slight odds defect where it was in their 
favour....no guarantee you would win, but when the odds get to be high 
enough, the bookies hedge fund can make much money.

The best bet were the idiots who took a bet on results for game in Australia 
after they had already occurred.  The matches were in Europe and for some 
reason - daylight savings, rain or whate have you - the matches had an early 
kick off.....but no one told the Aussie bookies who were still taking best 
after the fact.

JCE
```

```
languages. 'Small C' by Hendricks taught C by showing the assembler
code that it translated to.

> I just think you take it all too personally...

What makes you think that I take it personally at all ?
```

```
In article <i669f.32028$Pp1.6272@bignews3.bellsouth.net>,
 "Judson McClendon" <judmc@sunvaley0.com> wrote:

> Rick, both your issues involve misuse of features, not features that are 
> problems in and of themselves. At first glance it is obvious that use of '=' 
> vs. '==' is going to be mistaken by the human eye/brain, without a doubt. 
> Any programmer who denies *ever* being confused by C-style pointers either 
> hasn't used them much, or is lying. :-)

That is what testing is for.  C-style pointers are no more confusing 
than Pascal pointers, or Cobol pointers for that matter.

> It's not a clarity issue, but whoever selected colon-equal (:=) as an 
> assignment operator was insane. For such a common typed sequence to be not 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> (fact). :-)

I believe the ':=' was intended to evoke thoughts of Newton's arrow 
notation to indicate what we would call today "assignment".  (Or was 
that Leibnitz that came up with that...)

Either way, having a different operator to test for equity verses 
assignment is a really good idea.  Some languages, like BASIC, will use 
'=' for both and it can really get confusing.  That problem does not 
arise when you have "=" and "==", or "=" and ":=", or even "Equal" and 
"To/Giving".
```

```
are simply too visually similar. :-)
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<dk5f11$nlj$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<9OV3f.23230$S4.19969@edtnps84> <20051015132907.058$g2@news.newsreader.com> <pan.2005.10.16.03.08.37.117719@starband.net> <11l5em9nkca7318@news.supernews.com> <dj0guj0k2f@news4.newsguy.com> <hrn7l1daddpu1m368c3mpievfad47tt24v@4ax.com> <OZadnYRVUOPeKcveRVn-ig@adelphia.com> <4iz5f.37655$S4.20966@edtnps84> <1129839699.197305.4940@g44g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <djbj7a0h14@news3.newsguy.com> <c7d6f.5173$V67.1408@bignews6.bellsouth.net> <joe_zitzelberger-E34590.00352129102005@ispnews.usenetserver.com> <sVL8f.16208$_31.11321@bignews5.bellsouth.net> <11m7gjj39e62acf@corp.supernews.com> <i669f.32028$Pp1.6272@bignews3.bellsouth.net>`

```

"Judson McClendon" <judmc@sunvaley0.com> wrote in message 
news:i669f.32028$Pp1.6272@bignews3.bellsouth.net...

> It's not a clarity issue, but whoever selected colon-equal (:=) as an 
> assignment operator was insane. For such a common typed sequence to be not 
> only two characters, but one of them shifted as well - that's insanity.

My recollection of this was that the original Algorithmic Language, a 
methodology for describing algorithms not originally intended as a 
*computer* language but rather a language for use in scholarly journals, had 
a "left arrow" graphic as its assignment operator.   The colon-equal 
combination was intended as a direct representation of that graphic.  The 
designers might have chosen something else back in the 1950's when this 
decision was made -- for example, multipunch 12-8-6 followed by punch 
11resulting in "<-", but that wasn't the choice that was made when the 
decision to create a *compiler* for ALGOL (not the intent of the designers 
of the language!) was made.

Similar graphic-substitution methodologies were later used to allow people 
who didn't have access to APL keyboards to write APL programs.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
Ah, you glib devil, you.

> It's not a clarity issue, but whoever selected colon-equal (:=) as an 
> assignment operator was insane.

Well, if the likes of John Backus (who used it in BNF) and Niklaus

overrated.

> For such a common typed sequence to be not

programmers having to depress the shift key in order to type the
assignment operator...

Though, come to think of it, it takes me no longer to type : than
it does to type ;, since the shifting is ably handled by my left
hand while my right hand strikes the character key.  How odd.

> Reminds me of the QWERTY keyboard - purposefully designed to be inefficient 
> so the typists of the 1890's wouldn't overrun the machines of the day 
> (fact). :-)

Very likely myth, albeit a popular one which has been repeated by
many an authority.  See http://www.utdallas.edu/~liebowit/keys1.html.
```

- **References:** `<1129839699.197305.4940@g44g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <djbj7a0h14@news3.newsguy.com> <c7d6f.5173$V67.1408@bignews6.bellsouth.net> <joe_zitzelberger-E34590.00352129102005@ispnews.usenetserver.com> <sVL8f.16208$_31.11321@bignews5.bellsouth.net> <11m7gjj39e62acf@corp.supernews.com> <i669f.32028$Pp1.6272@bignews3.bellsouth.net> <dk60e1020v9@news1.newsguy.com> <6m2dm1hlcelpb25t5pqc0k947rt73unapk@4ax.com> <o8w9f.232746$qM5.12908@fe04.news.easynews.com> <5a4dm1t38i5g2f5fdhcdt7et4v58tinobl@4ax.com>`

```

"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message 
news:5a4dm1t38i5g2f5fdhcdt7et4v58tinobl@4ax.com...
>
> It's interesting that using Java, the standard is to sometimes use
> CamelCase and sometimes use all lowercase for what looks like the same
> word (converted to filename).

    The convention I'm familiar with is to use PascalCase for class names, 
ALL_CAPITALS for constants, and camelCase for everything else. It's a sort 
of "Hungarian notation" where the casing implies what kind of thing the 
named thing represents.

    That being said, as a Java programmer, I've never found code to be 
confusing because of the casing, even when I came across code which violated 
the above conventions (which is rare).

> or to sometimes
> find that case sensitivity is on and sometimes it is off.

    AFAIK, Java, the language, is case sensitive throughout. It's just that 
certain implementations are not case sensitive due to limitations of the 
underlying OS. E.g. when specifying a .java source file to be compiled by 
the Java compiler, on Windows you can give the file name in any case, but 
preferrably the filename should match the name of the primary class 
contained in that file exactly, including casing style.

    So if you declare a class named "Foo" in a file named "foo.java" instead 
of "Foo.java", this should fail to compile, but on Windows it succeeds.

    - Oliver
```

```

"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message 
news:q7bcm15ig9ro62q615s0maov891aa3i35g@4ax.com...

> Is there any language where comments are disallowed?

Depends on what you mean by "is" and "are".

Standard COBOL prohibited arbitrarily-placed comments in the ENVIRONMENT and 
DATA divisions, allowing them only in the comment-entries and the REMARKS 
paragraph in the  IDENTIFICATION division, and in the NOTE statement in the 
PROCEDURE division, prior to ANSI X3.23-1974.  That's one reason the "*" 
comment was such a widespread *extension* to '68-and-prior COBOL.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
In article <sVL8f.16208$_31.11321@bignews5.bellsouth.net>,
 "Judson McClendon" <judmc@sunvaley0.com> wrote:

> programmers pine for that type of impossible-to-misunderstand clarity. ROTFL

And what makes "TO" any clearer than "=", or "IS EQUAL TO" any better 
than "=="?  Once you have read the language reference for either 
language it is pretty clear either way.

Cobol, alone among languages, inverts equality tests in truly weird 
ways.  Consider the common Cobol-only idiom:

   If a is equal to b
      continue
   else
      --do a not equal to b logic--
   end-if

This is a code style that only a Cobol programmer could love...and it 
easily qualifies for the "obfuscated" label compared to the simple, 
clear, idiom used in every other programming language known to human 
kind.
```

```
And Perl's not even in the running for worst offender.  APL has
already been cited, and much as I enjoy APL I'll happily admit that
its reputation as a "write-only language" is not entirely undeserved.
Some other languages that are used for serious work (eg SML) must
look odd to the uninitiated.  And then there are the "esoteric
languages" like Befunge and Unlambda which are truly hostile to the
reader.

It's easy to prove that there's always a more-confusing language.
Befunge, for example, is only the simplest of a (countably) infinite
set of languages of its type.  More complicated Funges are derived by
adding dimensions to a lower-order Funge.
```

```

"Joe Zitzelberger" <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote in message
news:joe_zitzelberger-58F137.22362830102005@ispnews.usenetserver.com...
[snip]
> Cobol, alone among languages, inverts equality tests in truly weird
> ways.  Consider the common Cobol-only idiom:
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> kind.

It is most definitely not a "Cobol-only idiom": It is sometimes
found in C programs. (Especially those I have written!)

Since I am, admittedly, biased. Here is what Steve McConnell,
"Code Complete," 1993, pp 312-314, has to say.

     Follow these guidelines when writing _if_ statements.

     Write the nominal path through the code first; then
     write the exceptions. Write your code so that the
     normal path through the code is clear. Make sure
     that the exceptions do not obscure the normal path
     of execution. This is important for both readability
     and performance. ...

     Put the normal case after the _if_ rather than after the
     _else_. Put the case you normally expect to process
     first. This is in line with the general principle of putting
     code that results from a decision as close as possible
     to the decision.

Clearly, if the "nominal path" or "normal case" is to do
nothing, then either CONTINUE or a null statement should
follow the _if_ and the exception should follow the _else_.

McConnell then continues.

     Follow the _if_ with a meaningful statement. Sometimes
     you see code like the next example, in which the _if_
     clause is null.

     C Example of a Null _if_ Clause
     if ( SomeTest )
         ;
     else
         {
         /* do something */
         ...
         }

     Most experienced programmers would avoid code like
     this if only to avoid the work of coding the extra null line
     and the _else_ line. It looks silly and is easily improved
     by negating the predicate in the _if_ statement, moving
     the code from the _else_ clause to the _if_ clause, and
     eliminating the _else_ clause.

H'm, "[m]ost experienced programmers would avoid" implies
'some experienced programmers would not avoid' (ignoring
the inexperienced who may not recognize what "looks silly").
These "some" then are the exceptions, or might I suggest
exceptional programmers, for whom clarity is more important
than avoiding "the work of coding" or what "looks silly". <g>

Code written "so that the normal path through the code is
clear" and "that the exceptions do not obscure the normal
path of execution" hardly "qualifies for the 'obfuscated' label".
```

```
"Joe Zitzelberger" <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote:
> "Judson McClendon" <judmc@sunvaley0.com> wrote:
>>
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
> kind.

This COBOL programmer hates such constructs. :-)

Once again, Joe, this is poor programming, not a language characteristic.

I'm going to get some flack over this, but here goes. COBOL is a simple 
language, and I see this as a strength. Over the years there have been 
programmers who were productive in COBOL who would never master C/C++. I 
believe this has been a benefit, because it increased the potential COBOL 
workforce, lowering overall costs. I've known a number of such programmers 
(perhaps Richard thinks I'm one of them :-). But when someone is working 
close to their "intellectual ceiling," they're going to be a bit timid of 
more complex or difficult constructs. Some COBOL programmers apparently do 
not feel comfortable with NOT logic. They avoid using it, and teach (or 
instruct) others to avoid it as well. But there isn't anything in the COBOL 
language that encourages avoiding NOT logic.
```

```
Of course there is. It just that most of us don't often

     1$set ans85 flagstd(M) flagas"S"
     2 identification division.
     3 program-id. min-std.
     4 data division.
     5 working-storage section.
     6 1 a binary pic s9(4) value +1.
     7 1 b binary pic s9(4) value 0.
     8 procedure division.
     9 begin.
    10     if a < b
    11         continue
    12     else
    13         move b to a
    14     end-if
    15     if not (a < b)
* 553-S************
**    "NEGATED SIMPLE CONDITION" : non-conforming standard ANS85
    16         move b to a
    17     end-if
    18     stop run.
```

```
In article <sVL8f.16208$_31.11321@bignews5.bellsouth.net>, "Judson McClendon" <judmc@sunvaley0.com> writes:
> 
> Too bad COBOL doesn't have such crystal clear, never mistaken features such 
> as '=' vs '=='.

The various contributors to the language were too busy adding crystal-
clear, never-mistaken features such as abbreviated conditions and the
scope-closing behavior of ".".

> And the subtleties of C-style pointers makes COBOL 
> programmers pine for that type of impossible-to-misunderstand clarity.

Whereas pointers are never used incorrectly in COBOL.

C certainly has its problems.  Ritchie himself has claimed that
default fall-through in switch cases was a mistake.  Many people feel
that "=" for assignment is excessively dangerous.  The standard
library contains far too many functions which are difficult or
impossible to use safely.  And so on.  But comparisons with C do
nothing to demonstrate that COBOL - in the abstract, or as it is used
- does not have problems of its own.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol work?

_(thread depth: 13)_

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2005-10-30T10:30:31-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1130697031.378676.226740@g44g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<9gs5Zo$eflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>`
- **References:** `<9OV3f.23230$S4.19969@edtnps84> <c7d6f.5173$V67.1408@bignews6.bellsouth.net> <joe_zitzelberger-E34590.00352129102005@ispnews.usenetserver.com> <9gs5Zo$eflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>`

```
> But having such headers makes it easier to process such comments by
> a machine, er, a computer program.

It is no less easy to process a line with *author than one with author.

In many of my systems there are comments throughout the program which
are bounded by *<html> and *</html> tags which are extracted to create
the user documentation. These comments may be anywhere, including the
ID, and explain the business rules and how to operate the program.

By having the commentary next to the code it is much more likely to be
updated correctly and the extract to the user documentation is
automatic.

> One could even think of the
> compiler putting "Author", "Date-compiled" etc into the compiled
> program

The compilers that I use do put the date compiled into the file without
any need for a specific instruction to do this, they also put in the
compiler version.

My compiled programs include a copyright notice, a date last changed
and a version which is easily done by a COPY statement at the start of
W-S. The make file will rebuild this copied file each time any compiles
are done.  Marker values in this allow the date/time and version to be
extracted by a program so that version checks are automated.

My text editor also has a macro which adds to the source the current
date/time and latest version number from a 'version' file that contains
a summary of all the changes in the system. If the file is changed it
will be stamped with date and version and the make file will ensure
that it is compiled.

> One could even think of ....

Don't just think of it, we have been doing it for decades.

One program will process all the source files collecting the version/
date-time added there automatically by the text editor to produce a
version control list.  Another program can then be used on a client
site to ensure that the compiled programs match this list.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol work?

_(thread depth: 15)_

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2005-10-31T10:25:59-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1130783159.581922.202410@z14g2000cwz.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<9gwAGrRPflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>`
- **References:** `<9OV3f.23230$S4.19969@edtnps84> <9gs5Zo$eflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <1130697031.378676.226740@g44g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <9gwAGrRPflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>`

```
R>> and the extract to the user documentation is automatic.

>  User documentation? I doubt that the internal program documentation
is useful for the user.

I doubt that 'internal documentation' that is only intended for a
programmer is useful at all.  My documentation is stuff that is useful
to the user, such as the business rules that are implemented, and
options for the input and explanations of the output.  By having parts
of the user documentation next to the code that implements it this is
more likely (for me at least) to be updated when the code is changed,
and it is more likely to be checked to see that the code and
documentation are in agreement.

For example the program may have:

      *--Version: 4.72  19/12/04  11:05:32       <-- added by editor
macro
       IDENTIFICATION DIVISION.
       PROGRAM-ID.     pinvxxxx.
      *<title>
      * Print Invoices
      *<html>
      * Prints the Picking Slips, Invoices and Inter Branch Transfers.
      * This uses the form AR030/97.
      *
      *<h4>Changes to December 2004</h4>
      *
      * The Invoice Reference items on the delivery tab are now
      * printed higher and this will require the printers to be
      * readjusted to get a higher lineup.
      *
      *<h4> Changes to September 2002</h4>
      *
      *<ul>
      *<li> Printing of Prices is now controlled by the
      * <a href="#pprice">Price Printing option</a> which is set
      * in <a href="custmain.htm">Customer Maintenance</a>.
      *<li> ....
      *</ul>
      *</html>
       ... ...
       PROCEDURE DIVISION.
       ... ...
       Print-Item-Line.
             ...
      *<html>
      *<h4><a name="pprice">Print Prices</a></h4>
      *
      * Prices are printed on the Picking/Packing Slips for Retail
      * Customers (Customer type 'R') and where the
      * Customer has requested that these be printed which is
      * indicated by setting the
      * <a href="custmain.htm#pprice">Price Printing option</a>
      * to All (A) or Packing (P).
      *</html>
           IF ( Document-Type = Picking-Slip
             AND ( Cust-Price-Print = "A" OR "P"
                   OR Cust-Type = "R" )
                )
                 .....

Sometimes I have had to reorder the program paragraphs to get the user
documentation extracted in a meaningful way, but that is not an issue
as my style has no dependency on the order that the paragraphs have
(except the first).
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol work?

_(thread depth: 16)_

- **From:** "Oliver Wong" <owong@castortech.com>
- **Date:** 2005-10-31T20:12:07+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<rUu9f.67486$S4.48146@edtnps84>`
- **References:** `<9OV3f.23230$S4.19969@edtnps84> <9gs5Zo$eflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <1130697031.378676.226740@g44g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <9gwAGrRPflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <1130783344.969913.229930@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com>`

```

"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:1130783344.969913.229930@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com...
>r> It is no less easy to process a line with *author than one with
> r> author.
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> against the HR database ?

    This is actually an issue that has come up for me and my project in 
developing a program that can "understand" COBOL programs. If the author of 
the program has actually used the AUTHOR keyword, I can assume that the next 
line, in some way, identifies the author of the program. That is because the 
COBOL standard specified a certain use for the AUTHOR keyword.

    With a generic comment, if I see that the first word of a comment line 
is "author", I can't reasonably assume that the following words identify the 
author of the program in some way, or at least not with the same degree of 
certainty as in the previous situation. The problem now is that if the 
author chooses to encode authoring information at all, there are an infinite 
ways of doing so:

    * AUTHOR: Oliver

    * author: Oliver

    * Author: Oliver

    * <author name="Oliver"/>

    * @author Oliver

    * Written By: Oliver

    So what my program does is this: If it encounters the AUTHOR keyword, it 
can claim to know who the author is. Otherwise, it doesn't even bother 
trying to parse the comments and just lists the author as unknown.

    - Oliver
```

```
<CompilationUnit>
  <ProgramUnit>
```

```
Just out of curiosity, does your program handle "continued" AUTHOR information, 
e.g.

 Identification Division.
  Author.  Now
            is the time
             for all.
             Good men to come to the aid of their party?
 Date-Compiled. This is replaced by real date.
*Remarks.  Start here.

(The rules of the Standard require the continuation in the B-margin, but I 
*think* some compilers allow it in the A-margin. However, "hyphen-in-column-7' 
is NOT allowed)
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol work?

_(thread depth: 18)_

- **From:** "Oliver Wong" <owong@castortech.com>
- **Date:** 2005-10-31T22:00:07+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<Htw9f.68281$S4.57655@edtnps84>`
- **References:** `<9OV3f.23230$S4.19969@edtnps84> <9gs5Zo$eflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <1130697031.378676.226740@g44g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <9gwAGrRPflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <1130783344.969913.229930@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com> <rUu9f.67486$S4.48146@edtnps84> <uPv9f.1950$vc4.1555@fe05.news.easynews.com>`

```
"William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote in message 
news:uPv9f.1950$vc4.1555@fe05.news.easynews.com...
> Just out of curiosity, does your program handle "continued" AUTHOR 
> information, e.g.
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> "hyphen-in-column-7' is NOT allowed)

    I reformatted your program as so:
<code>
       Identification Division.
       Author.  Now
                 is the time
                  for all.
                  Good men to come to the aid of their party?
       Date-Compiled. This is replaced by real date.
      *Remarks.  Start here.
</code>

And it complains about a missing Program-ID. If I then change the program as

<code>
       Identification Division.
       Program-ID. ContinuedAuthorProgram.
       Author.  Now
                 is the time
                  for all.
                  Good men to come to the aid of their party?
       Date-Compiled. This is replaced by real date.
      *Remarks.  Start here.
</code>

This is the XML representation of the parse tree:

<CompilationUnit>
  <ProgramUnit>
    <IdentificationDivision>
      <ProgramIdParagraph programName="ContinuedAuthorProgram">
      </ProgramIdParagraph>
      <IdentificationDivisionParagraph>
        <AuthorParagraph>
          <CommentLine comment="Now&#x000d;&#x000a;is the 
time&#x000d;&#x000a;for all.&#x000d;&#x000a;Good men to come to the aid of 
their party?&#x000d;&#x000a;"/>
        </AuthorParagraph>
      </IdentificationDivisionParagraph>
      <IdentificationDivisionParagraph>
        <DateCompiledParagraph>
          <CommentLine comment="This is replaced by real 
date.&#x000d;&#x000a;"/>
        </DateCompiledParagraph>
      </IdentificationDivisionParagraph>
    </IdentificationDivision>
  </ProgramUnit>
</CompilationUnit>

    Where &#x000d; and &#x000a; are XML escape codes for the newline 
terminator on Windows (i.e. "\r\n"). It looks like the indentation 
information is lost, but newlines are not.

    - Oliver
```

```
> Eh? On what compiler did DATE-COMPILED have to be updated manually?

The Date-Compiled paragraph in the _source_ program has to be updated
manually if it is to have anything meaningful in it.  The compiler does
not update the source file and does not need anything at all in the
source to put the date/time in the compiler listing header and object
code.

Perhaps if you had a machine without a system clock then the
'date-compiled' paragraph may have some use if it was manually updated
each time.
```

```
Richard,
  I am a little confused.  I thought you used Micro Focus compilers (among - 
possibly - others).  With Micro Focus, the

 DATE-COMPILED

paragraph definitely gets filled *IN THE LISTING* (and this has nothing to do 
with the header or footers on listings).

I am 99% certain that all compilers that conform to the '89 Intrinsic Function 
Amendment (and probably most that don't) also put it in the "object code" - even 
when the Function Date-Compiled intrinsic function isn't used.  However, I don't 
think there is any ANSI/ISO requirement for this (as there is for filling in the 
Identification Division paragraph - when code).
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol work?

_(thread depth: 14)_

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2005-10-31T08:28:40-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<dk5gno$oo4$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<9OV3f.23230$S4.19969@edtnps84> <c7d6f.5173$V67.1408@bignews6.bellsouth.net> <joe_zitzelberger-E34590.00352129102005@ispnews.usenetserver.com> <9gs5Zo$eflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <joe_zitzelberger-404417.22294430102005@ispnews.usenetserver.com>`

```

"Joe Zitzelberger" <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote in message 
news:joe_zitzelberger-404417.22294430102005@ispnews.usenetserver.com...

> IBM's Cobol, to cite one example, always embeds the actual system date
> and time an executable is generated.  It is easily machine readable.
> The text paragraph is less than useless, since it might provide
> erroneous information.

There are actually *three* time/date values to worry about in compilation: 
the time at which DATE-COMPILED was processed; the time at which the 
date/time value is written into the object code file; and the time/date at 
which the object code file is created (more of an issue for multipass 
compilers than for single-pass compilers).

Unisys MCP COBOL compilers go to some trouble to make sure the time recorded 
in the listing and the time recorded in the code file are exactly the same. 
The codefile creation timestamp itself may differ slightly.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol work?

_(thread depth: 8)_

- **From:** Joe Zitzelberger <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com>
- **Date:** 2005-10-29T00:28:27-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<joe_zitzelberger-295F9D.00282729102005@ispnews.usenetserver.com>`
- **References:** `<9OV3f.23230$S4.19969@edtnps84> <20051015132907.058$g2@news.newsreader.com> <pan.2005.10.16.03.08.37.117719@starband.net> <11l5em9nkca7318@news.supernews.com> <dj0guj0k2f@news4.newsguy.com> <hrn7l1daddpu1m368c3mpievfad47tt24v@4ax.com> <OZadnYRVUOPeKcveRVn-ig@adelphia.com> <4iz5f.37655$S4.20966@edtnps84>`

```
In article <4iz5f.37655$S4.20966@edtnps84>,
 "Oliver Wong" <owong@castortech.com> wrote:

> "John Culleton" <john@wexfordpress.com> wrote in message 
> news:OZadnYRVUOPeKcveRVn-ig@adelphia.com...
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> in their (incontestable) opinion.

There is a common Cobol convention that attempts to remove as much  
meaningfulness from names as possible.

It is very common to find programs where EVERY declared variable is 
prefixed by "WS-".  The effect of such tautologies is only to reduce the 
namespace, and thus the possible meaningfulness, by three characters.

But my personal pet peeve is the idea that some people have that a 
number provides some form of uniqueness.  A common example, using 
paragraph names:

   1000-READ-FILE.
   2000-READ-FILE.

Often the defenders of such crap will staunchly oppose the clear and 
meaningful approach of:

   READ-MASTER-FILE.
   READ-TRANSACTION-FILE.

There was once a (somewhat) useful purpose behind both of these.  The 
data name prefix did help locate variables when you got your compile 
listing back after your deck was submitted.  And the numeric prefix on 
the labels helped maintain order when the only control structure 
available was the GO TO.  But that was a long time ago.

Cobol culture and standard practices do anything but encourage 
meaningful names, the encourage some level of meaninglessness in support 
of conventions that have had no practical purpose for 30 years or so.
```

```
   These number-prefixes are one of the main means of obfuscation in a

whoops, one has an error which is very difficult to find.
```

```
>   1000-READ-FILE.

>of conventions that have had no practical purpose for 30 years or so.

One other advantage of having a numeric prefix was the ability to type
in the TSO/SPF command

f p'#' 8 prev

This finds the previous occurrence of a number in column 8 - which
should be the paragraph name.

A klutz, but a useful one in programs with lots of comments.    A
smarter CoBOL aware editor might have a "find previous paragraph name"
command, but I'm unaware of such.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol work?

_(thread depth: 9)_

- **From:** "Judson McClendon" <judmc@sunvaley0.com>
- **Date:** 2005-10-29T12:01:35-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<TTN8f.16242$_31.8441@bignews5.bellsouth.net>`
- **References:** `<9OV3f.23230$S4.19969@edtnps84> <20051015132907.058$g2@news.newsreader.com> <pan.2005.10.16.03.08.37.117719@starband.net> <11l5em9nkca7318@news.supernews.com> <dj0guj0k2f@news4.newsguy.com> <hrn7l1daddpu1m368c3mpievfad47tt24v@4ax.com> <OZadnYRVUOPeKcveRVn-ig@adelphia.com> <4iz5f.37655$S4.20966@edtnps84> <joe_zitzelberger-295F9D.00282729102005@ispnews.usenetserver.com>`

```
"Joe Zitzelberger" <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote:
>
> It is very common to find programs where EVERY declared variable is
…[24 more quoted lines elided]…
> of conventions that have had no practical purpose for 30 years or so.

I believe you have a limited view, my friend. :-) The reasons above are not 
the only ones for those conventions. I do agree that preceding *every* 
WORKING STORAGE data item with "WS-" is nutty.

The convention today is to break applications down into very small programs, 
but sometimes there are practical reasons of system architecture that make 
fewer, larger programs desirable. In an online program that handles 30 or 50 
different screens, it makes perfect sense to prefix every paragraph that 
handles screen 12, for example, with 12nnnn-, and place '12' in the prefix 
of every WORKING STORAGE data item for that screen. In such a program there 
may be quite a number of READ-MASTER-FILE paragraphs, all very similar, 
though likely not identical, and the '12nnnn-' type of paragraph prefix 
identifies them perfectly. And if you have 5000 lines of DATA DIVISION, 
prefix of 'WS-' meaning a 77 level can be very useful. Sure, you can find 
any data item quickly with your source editor, but why do even that, if the 
name can tell what you need to know? This is so simple and powerful, I am 
amazed that it isn't very obvious to you.

I sometimes build systems with hundreds of thousands, even millions, of 
lines of code and many files. In such a system, I create name and 
abbreviation dictionaries so that every word used in data/paragraph names is 
spelled or abbreviated in *exactly* the same way in every use. Data names 
for the same field never vary, except for a prefix that designates the 
record (or "WS-" for 77 levels) containing the field, or when the sense of 
the field changes (e.g. Current Balance -> Previous Balance). My systems are 
crafted as carefully as I can build them. Nothing is left to chance that I 
can prevent, and I go to great lengths to make them understandable and 
maintainable. In my whole career (since '68) have never once delivered a 
system that a client was less than very happy with, and typically, after 
three or four weeks eliminating the few final bugs, I don't hear from the 
clients for months or years, until they want something new. Two weeks ago I 
fixed a bug in one of my systems that another programmer put there, and 
that's the first bug in a deployed system I've fixed for over a year, and 
that one was by the same programmer. If you have programming methods that 
produce better results than this archaic, out-of-date methodology that I 
use, then please tell me about them. :-)

Just because the reason for something isn't obvious to you, doesn't mean 
there isn't a reason, even a good one. The real world is messy and chaotic. 
Programming real-world applications so that they are clear and 
understandable takes hard work, discipline, and careful adherence to 
practical methodology, things we see too little of these days. :-)
```

```
> them understandable and maintainable.
Every author thinks that their novel is great.  Turns out it depends on the 
reader.  I happen to think Citizen Kane is a great movie - but I can find 
hundreds who find it tedious.  I applaud your efforts - and I do think it 
probably results in easier to understand code, but nothing will make a 
million lines of code easily maintainable.  I also believe that the hardest 
bugs to find are the ones that create systematically incorrect but 
consistent results.  Bug Free code does *not* exist even if you are Phi 
Kappa Gamma Six Sigma Black Belt with a CMM level 5 arm band development 
organization.
These you never find...they become "business rules" by accident after they 
are propagated to the regression test basis.

> In my whole career (since '68) have never once delivered a system that a 
> client was less than very happy with, and typically, after three or four 
> weeks eliminating the few final bugs, I don't hear from the clients for 
> months or years, until they want something new.
I've never seen a client that was anything but less than happy.  It's NEVER 
cheap or fast enough.  Your clients are negotiating weenies :-)

> Two weeks ago I fixed a bug in one of my systems that another programmer 
> put there, and that's the first bug in a deployed system I've fixed for 
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> whoever believes in Him should not perish but have everlasting life."
DU
```

```
I personally have written and/or designed some 70 different systems in about 
35 years. Deploying is a piece of cake, compared to design and writing, 
which is the real work. :-)

>> Data names for the same field never vary, except for a prefix that 
>> designates the record (or "WS-" for 77 levels) containing the field, or 
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> make a million lines of code easily maintainable.

Maybe you've never seen one done really well? One system I did in 1997, with 
one associate helper who was experienced, but a COBOL newbie, was about 2.5 
milllion lines of code. The old system, before our new system was installed, 
took 5 full time programmers, and most of another's time to maintain, and 
they were 6 months behind on changes. The new system takes only part of one 
programmer's time to maintain. And this is a system with frequent changes, a 
vehicle license system. Alabama has well over 200 types of vehicle licenses, 
and it only takes a few signatures on a petition to create a special 
license, so new ones pop up all the time. I wasn't even there the day they 
brought it up, I was at an important meeting kicking off a Y2K project at 
another client that day. Balanced to the penny that day and every day since. 
Contact me by email, and I will be happy to refer you to the responsible 
parties, if you want to check. :-)

>  I also believe that the hardest bugs to find are the ones that create 
> systematically incorrect but consistent results.  Bug Free code does *not* 
> exist even if you are Phi Kappa Gamma Six Sigma Black Belt with a CMM 
> level 5 arm band development organization.

I agree, nothing and nobody is perfect. But you might be very surprised how 
well it can be done when you work hard enough to do it right, and use the 
right methods. :-)

>> In my whole career (since '68) have never once delivered a system that a 
>> client was less than very happy with, and typically, after three or four 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> NEVER cheap or fast enough.  Your clients are negotiating weenies :-)

Well, I have had a few clients who weren't knowledgeable about software 
costs complain about the money. But once they hire another software vendor 
for something, that's the end of that. They wait as long as it takes, and 
don't complain about the cost. We worked our butts off for 18 months and 
billed a total of $408,000 for the vehicle license system above. The County 
Commission (not I.T.) complained about the cost, can you believe that? But 
they all love the system. :-)

Negotiating weenies? Hardly! One of my long time (since 1988) clients and I 
had a falling out last year, but it had nothing to do with software. I don't 
sell hardware. I often spec hardware, of course, but I don't sell it. He 
demanded that I suggest someone to buy hardware from. Under protest, I told 
him who I buy hardware from, insisting the choice was his responsibility. 
Then he tried to nail me when one of the 7 PCs had some hardware problems. 
He hardly speaks to me now. But he doesn't complain about the software I 
wrote for him, he loves that. FYI his client/server inventory system is over 
100k LOC, and I wrote it in 1988-89. From the day that system was installed 
he has never had to reload the database because of a program bug. I billed 
him $30,000 to write the system, but he complained so much about the six or 
eight bugs I fixed over the next few weeks, that I refunded $6,000. In his 
mind, it should install with zero bugs. That still makes me angry. But he 
loves the system. :-)

Both these system were done on a time & materials basis, not fixed bid.
```

```
> "Defaultuser" <Defaultuser@hotmail.com> wrote:
>> "Judson McClendon" <judmc@sunvaley0.com> wrote:
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…

I think that often deploying can be _more_ complicated than some software
design and writing.  A typical enterprise installation requires multiple
hardware systems, not one single point of failure (that is redundant
storage, cpus, databases, power, offices and device drivers) and that
doesn't even begin to discuss the need to determine the resources and people
needed to manage and run the operations after the deployment.  Depends
highly on the breadth of the application, of course, but when it is your end
to end supply chain....and that doesn't even touch on the maintenance and
management of the security to all the layers of the system...and that
doesn't even begin to touch on the management of the OS and software and
updates (even Linux has updates).

It ain't a piece of cake...I think unfortunately there is a tendency for
execs (this is not implying I think you are an exec, or that I even have you
are in mind - after all I don't know you) to think that you pay, you deploy,
you watch the cost savings without understanding what that means long term.
There are savings to be had, and more often than not, efficiencies to be
gained....but there is still a price for that and it's often in length of
contract, organizational restructure, business process realignment etc.

As that's not my thing at the moment though, I'm going to agree with you.

69444.45 LOC / month

2314.815 LOC/ day

2314.82  / 1440 =1.61

6-7

of every day for 18 months.
```

```
The last few months we had a third person who helped write some of the 
report programs, but the two of us wrote all the online programs.

When I'm really cranking can I sometimes write up to 4,000 LOC/hr by hand, 
though I can't maintain that rate all the time, of course. But the 
characteristics of this particular system allowed me to design it in such a 
way that I could partially automate certain parts of the coding for the 
online programs. Don't cringe, the result was exactly as if every line had 
been hand coded, only quicker and with fewer errors. :-)  I posted some of 
the techniques here several years ago in a discussion about exactly what 
constituted "writing code."
```

```
"Joe Zitzelberger" <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote in message 
news:joe_zitzelberger-295F9D.00282729102005@ispnews.usenetserver.com...
> In article <4iz5f.37655$S4.20966@edtnps84>,
> "Oliver Wong" <owong@castortech.com> wrote:
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>> in their (incontestable) opinion.

Actually - knowing some professors this is becoming less common.  Today's 
spoiled children do not like being told they are wrong.  This leads to two 
types of common questions: those with undisputable answers (what is 1 + 1 
using standard integer math as taught by me) and those that get marked as 
working or not working.  In fact these days you get given a set of "test 
cases" and it is the passing of those test cases that dictates how well you 
did...not any style.

> There is a common Cobol convention that attempts to remove as much
> meaningfulness from names as possible.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> namespace, and thus the possible meaningfulness, by three characters.

There can sometimes be benefit to using "WS-" versus "LS-" or "DB2-" or 
"IMS-"  "GL-" "EX-" to state the scope or source.  I find the benefit 
limiting because for some reason COBOL programmers are obsessive about data 
moves and it all ends up being modified everywhere anyway.
I'm not fussed about any naming convention as long as it's accurate. I don't 
care if someone uses
lpsz as a long pointer to a null terminated string or not....as long as 
that's what it is.  I don't ask for much, just consistency.

COBOL mentality...
SELECT INTO :DB-VALUE FROM DB2
MOVE DB-VALUE TO WS-VALUE

Not sure why it now needs to be in yet another variable, but whatever.  See 
it all the time.

> But my personal pet peeve is the idea that some people have that a
> number provides some form of uniqueness.  A common example, using
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>   2000-READ-FILE.

It also makes for amusement when making code changes.....
1000-READ-FILE
1500-READ-RECORD
1550-PROCESS-RECORD
1555-ADD-DATA-TO-RECORD
1555-POINT-5-ADD-ONE-MORE-THING

hmmmm...then (for the ISPF users)
C all 1000- 10000-
C all 1500- 15000-
C all 1550- 15500-
C all 1555- 15550-

C all 1555-POINT-5- 15555-

All fixed.

> Often the defenders of such crap will staunchly oppose the clear and
> meaningful approach of:
>
>   READ-MASTER-FILE.
>   READ-TRANSACTION-FILE.

That's good as long as that paragraph is reading the file and doing nothing 
more...otherwise it's misleading.  Paragraphs that state what they are doing 
shouldn't do more than that.

> There was once a (somewhat) useful purpose behind both of these.  The
> data name prefix did help locate variables when you got your compile
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> of conventions that have had no practical purpose for 30 years or so.
I was sarcastic with my statement about COBOL programmers.  I sense that you 
are not.  What documented standard practices are you referring to that do 
anything but encourage meaningful names? I think that it's more often to do 
with the "cut and paste" nature that exists in most development shops.  I've 
never written a prolog or identification division or file division in my 
life.  I copy them.  More than likely from someone who is "top-dog" in the 
office.

My personal pet peeve is the use of upper case to write all code.  I think 
that about 70% of reading is performed by "scanning" the shape of the 
letters.  Uppercase code is harder to read than lower case code which is why 
XML generally is mixed/lower case.

IF YOU DONT BELIEVE WHAT I AM SAYING THEN YOU SHOULD CONSIDER HOW MUCH 
HARDER IT IS TO READ THIS SHORT SENTENCE PART than it is to read this small 
sentence part.  The brain is a magical thing....shame we abuse it :-)


D
```

### Inspect...

- **From:** "EBille" <billard.eric@free.fr>
- **Date:** 2005-10-17T05:59:26-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1129553966.407455.254520@f14g2000cwb.googlegroups.com>`

```
Hello,
I'm looking for a solution to validate datas :
Suppose you got first-name and want to display error message when you
encounter two spaces (and some other characters).
For exemple 'MIKE DANIEL    ' is good (because there is only one space
between Mike and Daniel) but 'MIKE  DANIEL   ' is not. The problem is
trailing spaces. I have tried this :
inspect ws-firstname tallying ws-mytally for characters before '  '
but it doesn't works : first-name never validates because of trailing
spaces.  Anybody has a solution ?
Thanks,
EB
Grenoble
France
```

#### ↳ Re: Inspect...

- **From:** "Mike B" <mxbNoSpam@ev1.net>

```
"EBille" <billard.eric@free.fr> wrote in message
news:1129553966.407455.254520@f14g2000cwb.googlegroups.com
> Hello,
> I'm looking for a solution to validate datas :
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> spaces.  Anybody has a solution ?

I am not the world's greatest COBOL programmer, but I've recently had to 
solve a similar problem. I used COBOL reference modification and started at 
the back of the data, reading forward until I found the first non-blank 
character and then using a loop to the beginning of the field to check for 
two adjacent spaces.

If you can't figure it out, post back. I need to dash off now and don't have 
time to look for the code, but I'll check back this afternoon and post some 
code if you need it.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Inspect...

- **From:** "Mike" <MPBrede@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2005-10-17T08:23:49-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1129562629.617650.44890@z14g2000cwz.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<1129553966.407455.254520@f14g2000cwb.googlegroups.com> <4la7l1h93tejsjgupbr9vtco6tjd91if08@4ax.com>`

```

howard.brazee@cusys.edu wrote:
> On 17 Oct 2005 05:59:26 -0700, "EBille" <billard.eric@free.fr> wrote:
>
> >I'm looking for a solution to validate datas :
>
> "datas"?

You should forgive him, he is French. Data has a plural in French. ;)

>
> >Suppose you got first-name and want to display error message when you
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> reference modification loops here.

Here is a sample I wrote. Serves two purposes. Solves your problem and
I can also get some feedback on my way of writing COBOL. Be kind,
gentle reader. :-)
       IDENTIFICATION DIVISION.
       PROGRAM-ID.    NameTest.
       ENVIRONMENT DIVISION.
       DATA DIVISION.
       WORKING-STORAGE SECTION.
       01  GoodName                pic x(20) value 'Daniel Miller'.
       01  BadName                 pic x(20) value 'Daniel  Miller'.
       01  Ptr                     pic 9(02) value zero.
       01  Ptr2                    pic 9(02) value zero.
       01  EndLoopFlag             Pic x(01) value '0'.
           88  EndLoop                       value '1'.
           88  RunLoop                       value '0'.
       01  DblSpace                pic xx    value '  '.
       PROCEDURE DIVISION.

           If BadName not = space
               Perform varying ptr from length of BadName
                           by -1
                           Until EndLoop
                   if Badname(ptr:1) = space
                       Continue
                   else
                       Set EndLoop to true
                   End-If
               End-perform

               Set runloop to true
               Perform varying ptr2 from ptr
                           by -1
                           Until EndLoop
                   If BadName(ptr2:2) = DblSpace
                       Display "Name is rotten at " ptr2
                       set EndLoop to True
                   Else
                       if ptr2 = 0
                           Set EndLoop to true
                       End-if
                   End-if
               End-perform
           End-if

           Display 'End of Run'
           Stop Run.
```

```
On 17 Oct 2005 08:23:49 -0700, "Mike" <MPBrede@gmail.com> wrote:

>> "datas"?
>
>You should forgive him, he is French. Data has a plural in French. ;)

Interesting.   We use the Latin in English, where data *is* the plural
of datum.   While I knew that the French fight the inclusion of
foreign words and grammar, but didn't know it went back that far.    I
suspect it was a retro-fit.   Having a consistent grammar is
attractive to programmers such as myself.

But we're well in the process of forgetting that "data" is plural -
people in data processing rarely use the word "datum" and often say
"my data indicates that..."

But even worse, people in the media rarely use the word "medium".

I like the optional plural for "schema" of "schemata".   As in "I
modified some subschemata in our database".
```

```
The distinction between data and datum implies that data are in discreet 
units, which is not always true. I prefer the connotation that data is, at 
least potentially, not in discreet units, such as is implied in the names 
for liquids (e.g. milk, water), where there is no singular/plural, at least 
in English.
```

```

In article <r4u9l1dhi0c1gdiq2r1j2pjhre75i9kr65@4ax.com>, Ian Dalziel <iandalziel@lineone.net> writes:
> On Tue, 18 Oct 2005 08:17:13 -0500, "Judson McClendon"
> <judmc@sunvaley0.com> wrote:
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> Used any half bits lately?

Seriously, partial bits come up quite often in information theory,
compression, and other areas of study.  There's a surprising amount
of work done on trits (unbiased three-state entities), which encode
the same amount of information as lg(3) = 1.5849... bits.  I think
I've seen some references to work using notional information quanta
equivalent to e (= 2.7182...) bits - so there you have irrational
(even transcendental) partial bits.

While the bit is clearly the smallest discrete integral information
quantum, and thus "natural" in that sense, it's not the only
conceivable or useful information quantum.

Moreover, as I noted in another post, while digital computers by
definition work on discrete data, analog computers do not, and for
them using data as a continuous noun is quite sensible.
```

```
Judson McClendon wrote:

> The distinction between data and datum implies that data are in discreet
> units, which is not always true. I prefer the connotation that data is, at
> least potentially, not in discreet units, such as is implied in the names
> for liquids (e.g. milk, water), where there is no singular/plural, at least
> in English.

Sometimes data is discreet, and sometimes it is most indiscreet. It
depends on the subject matter. However a single item of information,
whatever the subject, is a discrete quantity or value.

are too young...
```
